\mathcal Y_{\rm eff}^{(0)}=L-KH_0^{-1}K^\dagger,
\]
and the higher powers of \(H_0^{-1}\) generate systematic subleading corrections.

\section{Kernel representation for the PDE problem}

Suppose the heavy sector is represented by a differential operator \(\mathbb H\) with Green kernel
\[
G_H(z,w;\lambda)=\langle z|(\mathbb H-\lambda)^{-1}|w\rangle.
\]
Let the light-heavy mixing kernel be \(\mathbb K(x,z)\).
Then the effective light kernel is
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal Y_{\rm eff}(x,y;\lambda)
=
L(x,y;\lambda)
-
\iint \mathbb K(x,z)\,G_H(z,w;\lambda)\,\mathbb K^\dagger(w,y)\,dz\,dw.
}
\]

Projecting onto the family basis \(\{\phi_a\}_{a=1}^3\), one gets
\[
(\mathcal Y_{\rm eff})_{ab}
=
\iint \phi_a^\dagger(x)\,\mathcal Y_{\rm eff}(x,y;\lambda)\,\phi_b(y)\,dx\,dy.
\]

Define the source profile induced by the \(a\)-th family mode:
\[
J_a(z;\lambda)
:=
\int \mathbb K^\dagger(z,x;\lambda)\,\phi_a(x)\,dx.
\]
Then
\[
\boxed{
(\mathcal Y_{\rm eff})_{ab}
=
L_{ab}
-
\iint J_a^\dagger(z;\lambda)\,
G_H(z,w;\lambda)\,
J_b(w;\lambda)\,dz\,dw.
}
\]

This is the general microscopic matrix-element formula.
It is the exact place where the reduced family coefficients come from.

\section{Finite heavy-mode expansion}

Let \(\{\chi_\alpha\}\) be an orthonormal heavy basis, so that
\[
H_{\alpha\beta}=\langle \chi_\alpha, \mathbb O\,\chi_\beta\rangle,
\qquad
K_{a\alpha}=\langle \phi_a,\mathbb O\,\chi_\alpha\rangle,
\qquad
L_{ab}=\langle \phi_a,\mathbb O\,\phi_b\rangle.
\]
Then
\[
\boxed{
(\mathcal Y_{\rm eff})_{ab}
=
L_{ab}
-
\sum_{\alpha,\beta}
K_{a\alpha}\,
(H^{-1})_{\alpha\beta}\,
\overline{K_{b\beta}}.
}
\]

Thus the entire reduced flavor problem has been reduced to the microscopic quantities
\[
L_{ab},\qquad K_{a\alpha},\qquad H_{\alpha\beta}.
\]

\section{Projection onto the canonical Hermitian basis}

We now project the effective \(3\times3\) Hermitian operator onto the canonical basis
\[
D_0=\mathbf 1,\qquad
D_1=\mathrm{diag}(1,-1,0),\qquad
D_2=\mathrm{diag}(1,1,-2),
\]
\[
S_{12}=E_{12}+E_{21},\qquad
S_{13}=E_{13}+E_{31},\qquad
S_{23}=E_{23}+E_{32},
\]
\[
A_{12}=i(E_{12}-E_{21}),\qquad
A_{13}=i(E_{13}-E_{31}),\qquad
A_{23}=i(E_{23}-E_{32}).
\]

Every Hermitian \(3\times3\) matrix \(M\) has the unique expansion
\[
M
=
\alpha_0 D_0+\alpha_1 D_1+\alpha_2 D_2
+s_{12}S_{12}+s_{13}S_{13}+s_{23}S_{23}
+a_{12}A_{12}+a_{13}A_{13}+a_{23}A_{23}.
\]

The coefficients are
\[
\alpha_0=\frac{1}{3}\Tr M,
\qquad
\alpha_1=\frac{1}{2}\Tr(MD_1)=\frac{M_{11}-M_{22}}{2},
\qquad
\alpha_2=\frac{1}{6}\Tr(MD_2)=\frac{M_{11}+M_{22}-2M_{33}}{6},
\]
\[
s_{12}=\frac{1}{2}\Tr(MS_{12})=\Re M_{12},
\qquad
s_{13}=\frac{1}{2}\Tr(MS_{13})=\Re M_{13},
\qquad
s_{23}=\frac{1}{2}\Tr(MS_{23})=\Re M_{23},
\]
\[
a_{12}=\frac{1}{2}\Tr(MA_{12})=\Im M_{12},
\qquad
a_{13}=\frac{1}{2}\Tr(MA_{13})=\Im M_{13},
\qquad
a_{23}=\frac{1}{2}\Tr(MA_{23})=\Im M_{23}.
\]

Applying this to
\[
M=\mathcal Y_{\rm eff},
\]
we obtain the exact reduced coefficients
\[
\boxed{
\alpha_i^{\rm eff},\ s_{ab}^{\rm eff},\ a_{ab}^{\rm eff}
}
\]
directly from the microscopic block data.

\section{Origin of the CP-odd coefficients}

From
\[
(\mathcal Y_{\rm eff})_{ab}
=
L_{ab}
-
\sum_{\alpha,\beta}
K_{a\alpha}(H^{-1})_{\alpha\beta}\overline{K_{b\beta}},
\]
it follows that
\[
a_{ab}^{\rm eff}
=
\Im L_{ab}
-
\Im\!\left[
\sum_{\alpha,\beta}
K_{a\alpha}(H^{-1})_{\alpha\beta}\overline{K_{b\beta}}
\right].
\]

Thus, if the bare light block \(L\) is real in the chosen family basis,
\[
\boxed{
a_{ab}^{\rm eff}
=
-
\Im\!\left[
\sum_{\alpha,\beta}
K_{a\alpha}(H^{-1})_{\alpha\beta}\overline{K_{b\beta}}
\right].
}
\]

Now assume \(H\) is positive definite.
Define the heavy-coupling vectors
\[
u_a := H^{-1/2}K^\dagger e_a \in \mathcal H_H,
\]
where \(e_a\) is the \(a\)-th standard basis vector of \(\mathbb C^3\).
Then
\[
\sum_{\alpha,\beta}
K_{a\alpha}(H^{-1})_{\alpha\beta}\overline{K_{b\beta}}
=
u_a^\dagger u_b.
\]
Hence
\[
\boxed{
(\mathcal Y_{\rm eff})_{ab}
=
L_{ab}
-
u_a^\dagger u_b,
}
\]
and therefore
\[
\boxed{
a_{ab}^{\rm eff}
=
\Im L_{ab}
-
\Im(u_a^\dagger u_b).
}
\]

This formula is conceptually decisive:
the CP-odd reduced coefficients are generated by the imaginary overlaps of the heavy-dressed coupling vectors \(u_a\).

\section{Reality criterion}

We now obtain a strict no-go criterion.

\paragraph{Proposition.}
If there exists a light/heavy basis in which
\[
L=L^\ast,\qquad
K=K^\ast,\qquad
H=H^\ast,
\]
then
\[
\mathcal Y_{\rm eff}=\mathcal Y_{\rm eff}^\ast,
\]
hence
\[
a_{12}^{\rm eff}=a_{13}^{\rm eff}=a_{23}^{\rm eff}=0.
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
If \(L,K,H\) are all real in the same basis, then \(H^{-1}\) is real as well, and
\[
KH^{-1}K^\dagger
\]
is real symmetric.
Therefore
\[
\mathcal Y_{\rm eff}=L-KH^{-1}K^\dagger
\]
is real symmetric.
Hence all imaginary off-diagonal coefficients vanish.
\(\square\)

So CP-odd reduced coefficients require a genuine microscopic complex obstruction.
This is an exact statement.

\section{How to identify } B_0,\ B_1,\ B_H \text{ from microscopic spurions}

At the microscopic level, suppose the full block operator depends on small spurions \(\epsilon_I\) (for example, sector-blind, hypercharge-sensitive, Higgs-like, anisotropy-like deformations):
\[
L=L^{(0)}+\sum_I \epsilon_I L^{(I)}+\mathcal O(\epsilon^2),
\]
\[
K=K^{(0)}+\sum_I \epsilon_I K^{(I)}+\mathcal O(\epsilon^2),
\]
\[
H=H^{(0)}+\sum_I \epsilon_I H^{(I)}+\mathcal O(\epsilon^2).
\]

Expanding the Schur complement to first order gives
\[
\mathcal Y_{\rm eff}
=
\mathcal Y_{\rm eff}^{(0)}
+
\sum_I \epsilon_I\,\mathcal Y_{\rm eff}^{(I)}
+
\mathcal O(\epsilon^2),
\]
with
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal Y_{\rm eff}^{(0)}
=
L^{(0)}-K^{(0)}(H^{(0)})^{-1}(K^{(0)})^\dagger,
}
\]
and
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal Y_{\rm eff}^{(I)}
=
L^{(I)}
-
K^{(I)}(H^{(0)})^{-1}(K^{(0)})^\dagger
-
K^{(0)}(H^{(0)})^{-1}(K^{(I)})^\dagger
+
K^{(0)}(H^{(0)})^{-1}H^{(I)}(H^{(0)})^{-1}(K^{(0)})^\dagger.
}
\]

This is the exact first-order formula one needs for the microscopic identification of
\[
B_0,\qquad B_1,\qquad B_H.
\]

For example, if the spurions are chosen as
\[
\epsilon_0=1,\qquad \epsilon_Y,\qquad \epsilon_H,
\]
corresponding respectively to the sector-blind, hypercharge-sensitive, and Higgs-like microscopic deformations, then one sets
\[
\boxed{
B_0=\mathcal Y_{\rm eff}^{(0)},
\qquad
B_1=\mathcal Y_{\rm eff}^{(Y)},
\qquad
B_H=\mathcal Y_{\rm eff}^{(H)},
}
\]
up to the normalization convention of the microscopic spurion parameters.

Thus \(B_0,B_1,B_H\) are not guessed textures.
They are the coefficients of the microscopic Schur-complement expansion.

\section{What is now rigorously closed}

The following facts are now exact at the reduced-operator level:

\begin{enumerate}
\item The reduced family operator is the Schur complement
\[
\mathcal Y_{\rm eff}=L-KH^{-1}K^\dagger.
\]

\item In the PDE picture, its matrix elements are
\[
(\mathcal Y_{\rm eff})_{ab}
=
L_{ab}
-
\iint J_a^\dagger G_H J_b.
\]

\item The Hermitian basis coefficients are read exactly by
\[
\alpha_0,\alpha_1,\alpha_2,\ s_{ab},\ a_{ab}.
\]

\item The CP-odd coefficients satisfy
\[
a_{ab}^{\rm eff}
=
\Im L_{ab}
-
\Im(u_a^\dagger u_b).
\]

\item If the microscopic data can all be made real in a common basis, then
\[
a_{12}^{\rm eff}=a_{13}^{\rm eff}=a_{23}^{\rm eff}=0.
\]

\item The first-order microscopic origin of
\[
B_0,\ B_1,\ B_H
\]
is given by the spurion-expanded Schur-complement formula above.
\end{enumerate}

\section{What remains open}

What is \emph{not} yet closed is the actual evaluation of the block data
\[
L^{(I)},\qquad K^{(I)},\qquad H^{(I)}
\]
from the specific TECT defect PDE and its heavy-sector spectrum.

That is the real next frontier.

The correct remaining task is therefore:
\[
\boxed{
\text{compute the microscopic matrix elements }L_{ab},\ K_{a\alpha},\ H_{\alpha\beta}
\text{ from the TECT defect background,}
}
\]
and then project them onto the basis
\[
\{D_i,S_{ab},A_{ab}\}.
\]
Only that step will decide which reduced operator channels are actually generated by the microscopic theory.

\section{Final summary}

We derived the correct microscopic origin of the reduced family flavor operator.

Given the light/heavy block decomposition
\[
\mathbb O=
\begin{pmatrix}
L & K\\
K^\dagger & H
\end{pmatrix},
\]
the exact reduced light-sector operator is the Schur complement
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal Y_{\rm eff}=L-KH^{-1}K^\dagger.
}
\]

In the PDE/kernel representation, its family matrix elements are
\[
\boxed{
(\mathcal Y_{\rm eff})_{ab}
=
L_{ab}
-
\iint J_a^\dagger(z)\,G_H(z,w)\,J_b(w)\,dz\,dw.
}
\]

Projecting onto the canonical Hermitian \(3\times3\) basis
\[
\{D_0,D_1,D_2,S_{12},S_{13},S_{23},A_{12},A_{13},A_{23}\},
\]
the coefficients are
\[
\alpha_0=\frac{1}{3}\Tr M,\qquad
\alpha_1=\frac{M_{11}-M_{22}}{2},\qquad
\alpha_2=\frac{M_{11}+M_{22}-2M_{33}}{6},
\]
\[
s_{ab}=\Re M_{ab},
\qquad
a_{ab}=\Im M_{ab}.
\]

Therefore the CP-odd carrier coefficients satisfy
\[
\boxed{
a_{ab}^{\rm eff}
=
\Im L_{ab}
-
\Im\!\left[
\sum_{\alpha,\beta}
K_{a\alpha}(H^{-1})_{\alpha\beta}\overline{K_{b\beta}}
\right].
}
\]

Equivalently, if \(H>0\) and \(u_a=H^{-1/2}K^\dagger e_a\), then
\[
\boxed{
a_{ab}^{\rm eff}=\Im L_{ab}-\Im(u_a^\dagger u_b).
}
\]

Hence the reduced CP structure is generated by the imaginary overlaps of the heavy-dressed coupling vectors.

We also derived the exact no-go statement:
if \(L,K,H\) can all be made real in a common basis, then
\[
a_{12}^{\rm eff}=a_{13}^{\rm eff}=a_{23}^{\rm eff}=0.
\]

Finally, if the microscopic operator depends on spurions \(\epsilon_I\), then the first-order reduced operators are
\[
\mathcal Y_{\rm eff}^{(0)}
=
L^{(0)}-K^{(0)}(H^{(0)})^{-1}(K^{(0)})^\dagger,
\]
\[
\mathcal Y_{\rm eff}^{(I)}
=
L^{(I)}
-
K^{(I)}(H^{(0)})^{-1}(K^{(0)})^\dagger
-
K^{(0)}(H^{(0)})^{-1}(K^{(I)})^\dagger
+
K^{(0)}(H^{(0)})^{-1}H^{(I)}(H^{(0)})^{-1}(K^{(0)})^\dagger.
\]

These are the correct microscopic formulas from which \(B_0,B_1,B_H\) must be extracted.

So the exploratory stage is over:
the next real task is to compute the microscopic block data
\[
L_{ab},\quad K_{a\alpha},\quad H_{\alpha\beta}
\]
from the actual TECT defect PDE and then project them onto
\[
\{D_i,S_{ab},A_{ab}\}.
\]

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Definition of the light family subspace and the correct microscopic computation pipeline}

We now formulate the reduced TECT flavor problem in a fully principled way.
The goal is to define the family light sector directly from the microscopic defect operator,
and then derive the reduced flavor matrices by block elimination rather than by texture ans\"atze.

\section{Microscopic reference operator and defect background}

Let
\[
\Phi_{\rm def}
\]
denote a static TECT defect background solving the appropriate microscopic field equations.
Let the microscopic one-particle operator on the corresponding background be
\[
\mathbb O(\epsilon)
=
\mathbb O^{(0)}+\sum_I \epsilon_I\,\mathbb V^{(I)}+\mathcal O(\epsilon^2),
\]
acting on a Hilbert space
\[
\mathcal H_{\rm micro}=L^2(\mathbb R^3)\otimes \mathcal H_{\rm int},
\]
where \(\mathcal H_{\rm int}\) denotes the internal/spinor/gauge fiber relevant to the reduced problem.

Here:

\begin{itemize}
\item \(\mathbb O^{(0)}\) is the reference sector-blind operator on the chosen defect background;
\item \(\mathbb V^{(I)}\) are microscopic deformations (hypercharge-sensitive, Higgs-like, anisotropy-like, etc.);
\item \(\epsilon_I\) are small bookkeeping spurions.
\end{itemize}

We assume throughout that \(\mathbb O^{(0)}\) is self-adjoint and that its low spectrum contains an isolated three-dimensional spectral sector.

\section{Spectral definition of the family light space}

Let
\[
\sigma_L=\{\lambda_1^{(0)},\lambda_2^{(0)},\lambda_3^{(0)}\}
\]
be three isolated low eigenvalues of \(\mathbb O^{(0)}\), separated from the rest of the spectrum by a gap
\[
\Delta_H>0.
\]
Let
\[
P_L
=
\mathbf 1_{\sigma_L}(\mathbb O^{(0)})
\]
be the spectral projector onto this low-energy sector, and define
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal H_L:=\operatorname{Ran}(P_L).
}
\]
Then
\[
\dim \mathcal H_L=3.
\]
This is the correct mathematical definition of the family space.

The heavy sector is the orthogonal complement
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal H_H:=\operatorname{Ran}(P_H),
\qquad
P_H:=1-P_L.
}
\]

This definition is basis-independent and does not rely on any Gaussian approximation.

\paragraph{Important point.}
The fundamental object is the projector \(P_L\), not a specific basis.
Any orthonormal basis
\[
\{\phi_1,\phi_2,\phi_3\}
\subset \mathcal H_L
\]
is admissible.
If one wants a localized family interpretation, one may choose a Wannier-type or defect-centered basis inside \(\mathcal H_L\),
but the reduced operator itself is defined independently of that choice.

\section{Localized family basis as a unitary gauge choice inside \(\mathcal H_L\)}

Let
\[
\{u_i\}_{i=1}^3
\]
be an orthonormal eigenbasis of \(\mathbb O^{(0)}|_{\mathcal H_L}\):
\[
\mathbb O^{(0)}u_i=\lambda_i^{(0)}u_i.
\]
A localized family basis is then any unitary rotation
\[
\phi_a=\sum_{i=1}^3 U_{ia}\,u_i,
\qquad
U\in U(3).
\]
Thus
\[
\{\phi_a\}_{a=1}^3
\]
can be chosen to optimize defect localization near three defect cores \(X_a\), but this is a basis choice inside the already well-defined light space.

So the logical order is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{first define }P_L\text{ spectrally, then choose a convenient basis } \phi_a \text{ inside } \mathcal H_L.
}
\]

\section{Exact light/heavy block decomposition}

Because \(P_L\) is a spectral projector of \(\mathbb O^{(0)}\), one has
\[
[\mathbb O^{(0)},P_L]=0,
\qquad
[\mathbb O^{(0)},P_H]=0.
\]
Hence the reference operator is exactly block-diagonal:
\[
\mathbb O^{(0)}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
L^{(0)} & 0\\
0 & H^{(0)}
\end{pmatrix}
\]
with respect to
\[
\mathcal H_{\rm micro}=\mathcal H_L\oplus \mathcal H_H.
\]

For the full operator
\[
\mathbb O(\epsilon)=\mathbb O^{(0)}+\mathbb V(\epsilon),
\qquad
\mathbb V(\epsilon)=\sum_I \epsilon_I \mathbb V^{(I)}+\mathcal O(\epsilon^2),
\]
the exact block form is
\[
\mathbb O(\epsilon)=
\begin{pmatrix}
L^{(0)}+V_{LL} & V_{LH}\\
V_{HL} & H^{(0)}+V_{HH}
\end{pmatrix},
\]
where
\[
V_{LL}=P_L\mathbb V P_L,\qquad
V_{LH}=P_L\mathbb V P_H,\qquad
V_{HL}=P_H\mathbb V P_L,\qquad
V_{HH}=P_H\mathbb V P_H.
\]

\section{Exact reduced operator and perturbative expansion}

The exact reduced light-sector operator is the Schur complement
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal Y_{\rm eff}
=
L^{(0)}+V_{LL}
-
V_{LH}\,(H^{(0)}+V_{HH})^{-1}\,V_{HL}.
}
\]

Expanding in small \(\epsilon_I\), one gets to second order
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal Y_{\rm eff}
=
L^{(0)}
+
V_{LL}
-
V_{LH}(H^{(0)})^{-1}V_{HL}
+\mathcal O(\epsilon^3),
}
\]
because \(V_{LL}\) is first order and \(V_{LH}(H^{(0)})^{-1}V_{HL}\) is second order.

Thus the first two nontrivial layers are:

\begin{itemize}
\item first-order direct light projection:
\[
\mathcal Y_{\rm eff}^{(1)}=V_{LL};
\]
\item second-order heavy-mediated correction:
\[
\mathcal Y_{\rm eff}^{(2)}=-V_{LH}(H^{(0)})^{-1}V_{HL}.
\]
\end{itemize}

This is the correct hierarchy.
The reduced flavor structure must be computed in this order.

\section{Definition of the matrix elements \(L,K,H\)}

Choose an orthonormal family basis
\[
\{\phi_a\}_{a=1}^3\subset \mathcal H_L
\]
and an orthonormal heavy basis
\[
\{\chi_\alpha\}_{\alpha\in \mathcal A}\subset \mathcal H_H.
\]

Then the microscopic block matrix elements are
\[
L^{(0)}_{ab}
=
\langle \phi_a,\mathbb O^{(0)}\phi_b\rangle,
\]
\[
V^{(I)}_{ab}
=
\langle \phi_a,\mathbb V^{(I)}\phi_b\rangle,
\]
\[
K^{(I)}_{a\alpha}
=
\langle \phi_a,\mathbb V^{(I)}\chi_\alpha\rangle,
\]
\[
H^{(0)}_{\alpha\beta}
=
\langle \chi_\alpha,\mathbb O^{(0)}\chi_\beta\rangle.
\]

Because \(\phi_a\in \mathcal H_L\) and \(\chi_\alpha\in \mathcal H_H\),
the reference light-heavy mixing vanishes identically:
\[
\langle \phi_a,\mathbb O^{(0)}\chi_\alpha\rangle=0.
\]

This is why the correct computation order is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{(1) solve the reference spectral problem,}
\quad
\text{(2) define }P_L,P_H,
\quad
\text{(3) compute only perturbative }V_{LL},V_{LH},V_{HH}.
}
\]

\section{Heavy eigenbasis and the second-order formula}

Now choose the heavy basis \(\chi_\alpha\) to diagonalize the reference heavy block:
\[
\mathbb O^{(0)}\chi_\alpha=E_\alpha \chi_\alpha,
\qquad
E_\alpha\ge \Delta_H>0.
\]
Then
\[
H^{(0)}_{\alpha\beta}=E_\alpha \delta_{\alpha\beta},
\qquad
(H^{(0)})^{-1}_{\alpha\beta}=E_\alpha^{-1}\delta_{\alpha\beta}.
\]

Therefore the second-order correction becomes
\[
\boxed{
(\mathcal Y_{\rm eff}^{(2)})_{ab}
=
-\sum_{I,J}\epsilon_I\epsilon_J
\sum_\alpha
\frac{
K_{a\alpha}^{(I)}\,
\overline{K_{b\alpha}^{(J)}}
}{E_\alpha}.
}
\]

In particular, if one temporarily focuses on a single perturbation channel \(\mathbb V\), then
\[
\boxed{
(\mathcal Y_{\rm eff}^{(2)})_{ab}
=
-
\sum_\alpha
\frac{
K_{a\alpha}\overline{K_{b\alpha}}
}{E_\alpha}.
}
\]

This is the microscopic formula that must be evaluated in the TECT defect background.

\section{Coordinate-space formulas}

If the operator acts on wavefunctions in space, then
\[
V^{(I)}_{ab}
=
\int d^3x\,
\phi_a^\dagger(x)\,
\bigl(\mathbb V^{(I)}\phi_b\bigr)(x),
\]
\[
K^{(I)}_{a\alpha}
=
\int d^3x\,
\phi_a^\dagger(x)\,
\bigl(\mathbb V^{(I)}\chi_\alpha\bigr)(x).
\]

For a local potential-type deformation
\[
\mathbb V^{(I)}=U_I(x),
\]
these reduce to
\[
V^{(I)}_{ab}
=
\int d^3x\,
\phi_a^\dagger(x)\,U_I(x)\,\phi_b(x),
\qquad
K^{(I)}_{a\alpha}
=
\int d^3x\,
\phi_a^\dagger(x)\,U_I(x)\,\chi_\alpha(x).
\]

For a first-order Dirac-type perturbation
\[
\mathbb V^{(I)}=\Gamma^i A_i^{(I)}(x)+M_I(x),
\]
one has
\[
V^{(I)}_{ab}
=
\int d^3x\,
\phi_a^\dagger(x)\,
\bigl(\Gamma^i A_i^{(I)}(x)+M_I(x)\bigr)\phi_b(x),
\]
and similarly for \(K^{(I)}_{a\alpha}\).
All derivative terms must be treated with the Hermitian domain conditions of the microscopic operator.

\section{Projection onto the Hermitian family basis}

After computing
\[
M:=\mathcal Y_{\rm eff}|_{\mathcal H_L},
\]
one expands it in the canonical Hermitian basis
\[
\{D_0,D_1,D_2,S_{12},S_{13},S_{23},A_{12},A_{13},A_{23}\}.
\]

The coefficients are
\[
\alpha_0=\frac{1}{3}\Tr M,
\qquad
\alpha_1=\frac{M_{11}-M_{22}}{2},
\qquad
\alpha_2=\frac{M_{11}+M_{22}-2M_{33}}{6},
\]
\[
s_{12}=\Re M_{12},
\qquad
s_{13}=\Re M_{13},
\qquad
s_{23}=\Re M_{23},
\]
\[
a_{12}=\Im M_{12},
\qquad
a_{13}=\Im M_{13},
\qquad
a_{23}=\Im M_{23}.
\]

Thus the actual computational pipeline is

\[
\boxed{
\Phi_{\rm def}
\;\to\;
\mathbb O^{(0)}
\;\to\;
P_L,P_H
\;\to\;
\phi_a,\chi_\alpha
\;\to\;
V_{ab}^{(I)},K_{a\alpha}^{(I)},E_\alpha
\;\to\;
\mathcal Y_{\rm eff}
\;\to\;
(\alpha_i,s_{ab},a_{ab}).
}
\]

\section{Microscopic origin of the CP-odd coefficients}

From the second-order formula,
\[
(\mathcal Y_{\rm eff}^{(2)})_{ab}
=
-
\sum_{I,J}\epsilon_I\epsilon_J
\sum_\alpha
\frac{
K_{a\alpha}^{(I)}\overline{K_{b\alpha}^{(J)}}
}{E_\alpha},
\]
it follows immediately that
\[
\boxed{
a_{ab}^{(2)}
=
-\sum_{I,J}\epsilon_I\epsilon_J
\sum_\alpha
\frac{
\Im\!\left(
K_{a\alpha}^{(I)}\overline{K_{b\alpha}^{(J)}}
\right)
}{E_\alpha}.
}
\]

This formula is decisive.
It shows that the reduced CP-odd coefficients do not come from vague ``complex textures'',
but from microscopic phase obstructions in the heavy-mediated overlap products
\[
K_{a\alpha}^{(I)}\overline{K_{b\alpha}^{(J)}}.
\]

Therefore:

\paragraph{Exact criterion.}
If there exists a common basis in which all
\[
K_{a\alpha}^{(I)}
\]
are real and all \(V_{ab}^{(I)}\) are real, then
\[
a_{12}=a_{13}=a_{23}=0
\]
to the corresponding perturbative order.

So CP-odd reduced coefficients require a genuine microscopic complex obstruction.

\section{Identification of } B_0,\ B_1,\ B_H

Suppose the perturbations are grouped as
\[
\mathbb V
=
\epsilon_0\,\mathbb V^{(0)}
+
\epsilon_Y\,\mathbb V^{(Y)}
+
\epsilon_H\,\mathbb V^{(H)}
+\cdots.
\]

Then the reduced effective operator admits the perturbative decomposition
\[
\mathcal Y_{\rm eff}
=
L^{(0)}
+
\epsilon_0\,\mathcal B_0^{(1)}
+
\epsilon_Y\,\mathcal B_1^{(1)}
+
\epsilon_H\,\mathcal B_H^{(1)}
+
\mathcal Q^{(2)}
+\cdots,
\]
where
\[
\mathcal B_0^{(1)}=P_L\mathbb V^{(0)}P_L,
\qquad
\mathcal B_1^{(1)}=P_L\mathbb V^{(Y)}P_L,
\qquad
\mathcal B_H^{(1)}=P_L\mathbb V^{(H)}P_L,
\]
and
\[
\mathcal Q^{(2)}
=
-
\sum_{I,J}
\epsilon_I\epsilon_J
P_L\mathbb V^{(I)}P_H
(H^{(0)})^{-1}
P_H\mathbb V^{(J)}P_L.
\]

So, microscopically, the objects previously called \(B_0,B_1,B_H\) are not guessed matrices.
They are the projected first-order spurion responses together with the systematic heavy-mediated corrections.

\section{What is rigorously closed}

The following items are now exact:

\begin{enumerate}
\item The family space is the isolated three-dimensional spectral subspace
\[
\mathcal H_L=\operatorname{Ran}\mathbf 1_{\sigma_L}(\mathbb O^{(0)}).
\]

\item A localized family basis is only a \(U(3)\) gauge choice inside \(\mathcal H_L\).

\item The exact reduced operator is the Schur complement
\[
\mathcal Y_{\rm eff}
=
L^{(0)}+V_{LL}-V_{LH}(H^{(0)}+V_{HH})^{-1}V_{HL}.
\]

\item To second order,
\[
\mathcal Y_{\rm eff}
=
L^{(0)}+V_{LL}-V_{LH}(H^{(0)})^{-1}V_{HL}+\mathcal O(\epsilon^3).
\]

\item In the heavy eigenbasis,
\[
(\mathcal Y_{\rm eff}^{(2)})_{ab}
=
-
\sum_{I,J}\epsilon_I\epsilon_J
\sum_\alpha
\frac{K_{a\alpha}^{(I)}\overline{K_{b\alpha}^{(J)}}}{E_\alpha}.
\]

\item Therefore the reduced CP-odd coefficients satisfy
\[
a_{ab}^{(2)}
=
-\sum_{I,J}\epsilon_I\epsilon_J
\sum_\alpha
\frac{\Im\!\left(K_{a\alpha}^{(I)}\overline{K_{b\alpha}^{(J)}}\right)}{E_\alpha}.
\]
\end{enumerate}

\section{What remains open}

What is not yet closed is the explicit evaluation of
\[
V_{ab}^{(I)},\qquad K_{a\alpha}^{(I)},\qquad E_\alpha
\]
for the actual TECT defect PDE and its heavy-mode spectrum.

That is now the correct next frontier.
The remaining work is no longer conceptual but computational:
solve the defect spectral problem, construct the light projector, compute the overlap matrix elements, and project the resulting \(\mathcal Y_{\rm eff}\) onto the Hermitian family basis.

\section{Final summary}

The reduced TECT flavor problem must be organized around the spectral projector of the microscopic reference defect operator.

The correct family space is
\[
\mathcal H_L=\operatorname{Ran}\mathbf 1_{\sigma_L}(\mathbb O^{(0)}),
\]
where \(\sigma_L\) is an isolated three-dimensional low-energy spectral sector.
A localized family basis \(\{\phi_a\}\) is then only a unitary choice inside this fixed space.

With respect to the decomposition
\[
\mathcal H_{\rm micro}=\mathcal H_L\oplus\mathcal H_H,
\]
the full operator takes the block form
\[
\mathbb O=
\begin{pmatrix}
L^{(0)}+V_{LL} & V_{LH}\\
V_{HL} & H^{(0)}+V_{HH}
\end{pmatrix},
\]
and the exact reduced family operator is
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal Y_{\rm eff}
=
L^{(0)}+V_{LL}-V_{LH}(H^{(0)}+V_{HH})^{-1}V_{HL}.
}
\]

To second order in the perturbations,
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal Y_{\rm eff}
=
L^{(0)}+V_{LL}-V_{LH}(H^{(0)})^{-1}V_{HL}+\mathcal O(\epsilon^3).
}
\]

If the heavy block is diagonalized as
\[
\mathbb O^{(0)}\chi_\alpha=E_\alpha\chi_\alpha,
\]
then
\[
(\mathcal Y_{\rm eff}^{(2)})_{ab}
=
-
\sum_{I,J}\epsilon_I\epsilon_J
\sum_\alpha
\frac{K_{a\alpha}^{(I)}\overline{K_{b\alpha}^{(J)}}}{E_\alpha},
\]
so the reduced CP-odd coefficients are
\[
\boxed{
a_{ab}^{(2)}
=
-\sum_{I,J}\epsilon_I\epsilon_J
\sum_\alpha
\frac{\Im\!\left(K_{a\alpha}^{(I)}\overline{K_{b\alpha}^{(J)}}\right)}{E_\alpha}.
}
\]

Therefore the true microscopic origin of reduced CP structure is the imaginary heavy-mediated overlap of the light-heavy couplings.

The correct computational order is:
\[
\boxed{
\Phi_{\rm def}
\to
\mathbb O^{(0)}
\to
P_L,P_H
\to
\phi_a,\chi_\alpha
\to
V_{ab}^{(I)},K_{a\alpha}^{(I)},E_\alpha
\to
\mathcal Y_{\rm eff}
\to
(D_i,S_{ab},A_{ab})\text{ coefficients}.
}
\]

Hence the next real task is no longer exploratory fitting.
It is the explicit evaluation of
\[
V_{ab}^{(I)},\quad K_{a\alpha}^{(I)},\quad E_\alpha
\]
from the actual TECT defect PDE and heavy-mode spectrum.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Construction of the light family projector from single-defect modes}

We now give the principled construction of the reduced family space directly from the microscopic reference operator on a multi-defect background.

\section{Reference operator on a single defect}

Let
\[
\Phi_{\rm 1def}
\]
be a single static TECT defect background centered at the origin, and let
\[
\mathbb O_{\rm 1def}^{(0)}
\]
be the corresponding self-adjoint reference operator on
\[
\mathcal H_{\rm micro}=L^2(\mathbb R^3)\otimes \mathcal H_{\rm int}.
\]

We assume that the single-defect problem has an isolated low-energy bound state (or quasi-zero mode)
\[
\psi_\star\in \mathcal H_{\rm micro},
\qquad
\mathbb O_{\rm 1def}^{(0)}\psi_\star=\lambda_\star \psi_\star,
\]
with
\[
\|\psi_\star\|=1,
\]
and that the rest of the spectrum is separated by a gap
\[
\Delta_H>0.
\]

The simplest family scenario is the rank-one case:
\[
\boxed{
\dim \ker_{\rm low}\bigl(\mathbb O_{\rm 1def}^{(0)}-\lambda_\star\bigr)=1.
}
\]
If a single defect carried \(q>1\) protected low modes, then a three-defect cluster would produce \(3q\) light states.
Hence the observed family dimension \(3\) naturally corresponds to the rank-one single-defect case.

\section{Translated defect modes}

Let \(X_1,X_2,X_3\in \mathbb R^3\) be three well-separated defect centers.
Assume that the three-defect background
\[
\Phi_{\rm 3def}
\]
is a weakly interacting superposition of three single defects centered at the points \(X_a\).

Define the translated approximate bound states
\[
\psi_a(x):=\mathcal T_{X_a}\psi_\star(x)=\psi_\star(x-X_a),
\qquad a=1,2,3.
\]
If the internal bundle has a gauge/spin connection, then one should more properly use parallel-transported translates
\[
\psi_a(x)=U_a(x)\,\psi_\star(x-X_a),
\]
where \(U_a\) is the appropriate microscopic transport operator.
For the abstract construction, this refinement does not change the algebra.

Because \(\psi_\star\) is exponentially localized,
\[
|\psi_\star(x)|\lesssim e^{-\mu |x|},
\]
the overlaps satisfy
\[
\langle \psi_a,\psi_b\rangle = \delta_{ab}+\mathcal O(e^{-\mu R_{ab}}),
\qquad
R_{ab}:=|X_a-X_b|.
\]

Thus the Gram matrix
\[
G_{ab}:=\langle \psi_a,\psi_b\rangle
\]
obeys
\[
G=I+\mathcal O(e^{-\mu R_{\min}}),
\qquad
R_{\min}:=\min_{a\neq b}R_{ab},
\]
and is invertible for sufficiently large separation.

\section{Approximate low subspace of the three-defect operator}

Let
\[
\mathbb O_{\rm 3def}^{(0)}
\]
be the microscopic reference operator on the three-defect background.
Define the span of the translated single-defect modes
\[
\mathcal S_{\rm app}:=\operatorname{span}\{\psi_1,\psi_2,\psi_3\}.
\]

The first reduced matrix on this approximate subspace is
\[
M_{ab}^{\rm app}:=\langle \psi_a,\mathbb O_{\rm 3def}^{(0)}\psi_b\rangle.
\]

Because each \(\psi_a\) is an approximate localized eigenmode near the \(a\)-th defect,
one expects
\[
M_{ab}^{\rm app}
=
\lambda_\star G_{ab}+T_{ab},
\]
where
\[
T_{ab}=\mathcal O(e^{-\mu R_{ab}})
\]
encodes the tunneling / overlap correction.

If \(\lambda_\star=0\), then
\[
M^{\rm app}=T
\]
is the effective tight-binding matrix of the low cluster.

\section{Orthonormalized localized basis}

Since the \(\psi_a\) are not exactly orthonormal, define
\[
\phi_a:=\sum_{b=1}^3 \psi_b\,(G^{-1/2})_{ba}.
\]
Then
\[
\langle \phi_a,\phi_b\rangle=\delta_{ab}.
\]

The projected approximate light matrix becomes
\[
L^{\rm app}:=G^{-1/2}M^{\rm app}G^{-1/2},
\]
that is,
\[
L^{\rm app}_{ab}
=
\langle \phi_a,\mathbb O_{\rm 3def}^{(0)}\phi_b\rangle.
\]

This is the canonical orthonormalized localized family basis associated with the three-defect configuration.

\section{Exact spectral projector and its relation to the approximate span}

The true light family space is not merely \(\mathcal S_{\rm app}\), but the exact low spectral subspace of \(\mathbb O_{\rm 3def}^{(0)}\).

Let \(\Gamma\) be a contour in the complex plane enclosing only the three low eigenvalues associated with the defect cluster and no other spectrum.
Then the exact light projector is the Riesz projector
\[
\boxed{
P_L
=
\frac{1}{2\pi i}
\oint_\Gamma
(z-\mathbb O_{\rm 3def}^{(0)})^{-1}\,dz.
}
\]

Define
\[
\mathcal H_L:=\operatorname{Ran}(P_L).
\]
Then
\[
\dim \mathcal H_L=3
\]
provided the three clustered eigenvalues remain isolated from the heavy spectrum.

Under standard exponentially localized multi-center perturbation theory, one has
\[
\|P_L-P_{\rm app}\|
\lesssim e^{-\mu R_{\min}},
\]
where \(P_{\rm app}\) is the orthogonal projector onto \(\mathcal S_{\rm app}\).
Hence the approximate span of translated single-defect modes converges exponentially to the exact light family space.

This is the precise meaning of the statement that the family sector is generated by three defect-localized modes.

\section{Basis-independence and localized gauge choice}

The family space is the exact subspace
\[
\mathcal H_L=\operatorname{Ran}(P_L),
\]
which is basis-independent.
A localized family basis is any orthonormal basis of \(\mathcal H_L\) chosen to maximize localization near the three defect cores.

In practice, one may use either:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the orthonormalized translated modes \(\phi_a\) above;
\item the eigenbasis of \(P_L\,\hat x\,P_L\) (or of another localization functional);
\item a Wannier-type minimization inside \(\mathcal H_L\).
\end{enumerate}

All such choices are related by a unitary matrix in \(U(3)\), and therefore define the same reduced operator up to family-basis conjugation.

\section{Heavy sector and exact block splitting}

Let
\[
P_H:=1-P_L,
\qquad
\mathcal H_H:=\operatorname{Ran}(P_H).
\]
Then, with respect to
\[
\mathcal H_{\rm micro}=\mathcal H_L\oplus \mathcal H_H,
\]
the reference operator is exactly block-diagonal:
\[
\mathbb O_{\rm 3def}^{(0)}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
L^{(0)} & 0\\
0 & H^{(0)}
\end{pmatrix},
\]
where
\[
L^{(0)}:=P_L\mathbb O_{\rm 3def}^{(0)}P_L,
\qquad
H^{(0)}:=P_H\mathbb O_{\rm 3def}^{(0)}P_H.
\]

If the three low eigenvalues are nearly degenerate, then in a localized basis \(\{\phi_a\}\),
\[
L^{(0)}_{ab}=\langle \phi_a,\mathbb O_{\rm 3def}^{(0)}\phi_b\rangle
\]
is exactly the reference family matrix induced by defect tunneling.

\section{Perturbations and the correct computation order}

Now introduce microscopic deformations
\[
\mathbb V(\epsilon)=\sum_I \epsilon_I \mathbb V^{(I)}+\mathcal O(\epsilon^2),
\qquad
\mathbb O(\epsilon)=\mathbb O_{\rm 3def}^{(0)}+\mathbb V(\epsilon).
\]

The correct block matrix elements are
\[
V_{LL}^{(I)}:=P_L\mathbb V^{(I)}P_L,
\qquad
V_{LH}^{(I)}:=P_L\mathbb V^{(I)}P_H,
\qquad
V_{HL}^{(I)}:=P_H\mathbb V^{(I)}P_L,
\qquad
V_{HH}^{(I)}:=P_H\mathbb V^{(I)}P_H.
\]

Choose an orthonormal localized family basis \(\{\phi_a\}\subset\mathcal H_L\) and an orthonormal heavy basis \(\{\chi_\alpha\}\subset\mathcal H_H\).
Then
\[
V_{ab}^{(I)}=\langle \phi_a,\mathbb V^{(I)}\phi_b\rangle,
\qquad
K_{a\alpha}^{(I)}=\langle \phi_a,\mathbb V^{(I)}\chi_\alpha\rangle,
\qquad
H_{\alpha\beta}^{(0)}=\langle \chi_\alpha,\mathbb O_{\rm 3def}^{(0)}\chi_\beta\rangle.
\]

The exact reduced operator is
\[
\mathcal Y_{\rm eff}
=
L^{(0)}+V_{LL}
-
V_{LH}(H^{(0)}+V_{HH})^{-1}V_{HL}.
\]

To second order,
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal Y_{\rm eff}
=
L^{(0)}
+
V_{LL}
-
V_{LH}(H^{(0)})^{-1}V_{HL}
+\mathcal O(\epsilon^3).
}
\]

So the correct computational pipeline is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{(1) solve the reference three-defect spectral problem,}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{(2) construct }P_L\text{ and a localized basis }\phi_a,
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{(3) compute }V_{ab}^{(I)}\text{ and }K_{a\alpha}^{(I)},
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{(4) evaluate }\mathcal Y_{\rm eff},
\text{ then project to }D_i,S_{ab},A_{ab}.
}
\]

\section{Heavy-eigenbasis formula}

If the heavy basis diagonalizes the reference heavy block,
\[
\mathbb O_{\rm 3def}^{(0)}\chi_\alpha=E_\alpha \chi_\alpha,
\qquad
E_\alpha\ge \Delta_H>0,
\]
then
\[
(H^{(0)})^{-1}_{\alpha\beta}=E_\alpha^{-1}\delta_{\alpha\beta},
\]
and the second-order reduced matrix is
\[
\boxed{
(\mathcal Y_{\rm eff}^{(2)})_{ab}
=
-
\sum_{I,J}\epsilon_I\epsilon_J
\sum_\alpha
\frac{K_{a\alpha}^{(I)}\overline{K_{b\alpha}^{(J)}}}{E_\alpha}.
}
\]

Hence the CP-odd reduced coefficients are
\[
\boxed{
a_{ab}^{(2)}
=
-
\sum_{I,J}\epsilon_I\epsilon_J
\sum_\alpha
\frac{
\Im\!\left(K_{a\alpha}^{(I)}\overline{K_{b\alpha}^{(J)}}\right)
}{E_\alpha}.
}
\]

This is the exact microscopic source of CP structure in the reduced family operator.

\section{What is rigorously closed and what is not}

The following points are now rigorously closed:

\begin{enumerate}
\item The family space is the exact low spectral subspace
\[
\mathcal H_L=\operatorname{Ran}(P_L)
\]
of the reference three-defect operator.

\item A localized family basis is a unitary gauge choice inside \(\mathcal H_L\), not an additional assumption.

\item For well-separated defects, the approximate span of translated single-defect modes converges exponentially to the exact family space.

\item The reduced operator is obtained by Schur complement after this spectral decomposition, not before it.

\item The CP-odd reduced coefficients arise from the imaginary heavy-mediated overlaps
\[
\Im\!\left(K_{a\alpha}^{(I)}\overline{K_{b\alpha}^{(J)}}\right).
\]
\end{enumerate}

What is not yet closed is the concrete TECT-specific input:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the actual form of \(\mathbb O_{\rm 1def}^{(0)}\) and \(\mathbb O_{\rm 3def}^{(0)}\);
\item the proof that a single defect carries exactly one protected light mode;
\item the size of the heavy gap \(\Delta_H\);
\item the explicit overlap integrals \(V_{ab}^{(I)}\) and \(K_{a\alpha}^{(I)}\).
\end{enumerate}

These require the explicit TECT defect PDE.

\section{Final summary}

The correct family-space construction in the reduced TECT flavor problem is spectral, not ansatz-based.

Start from the reference three-defect operator
\[
\mathbb O_{\rm 3def}^{(0)}
\]
and define the exact light family projector by the Riesz formula
\[
\boxed{
P_L=\frac{1}{2\pi i}\oint_\Gamma (z-\mathbb O_{\rm 3def}^{(0)})^{-1}dz,
}
\]
where \(\Gamma\) encloses the isolated three-state low-energy cluster.

Then
\[
\mathcal H_L=\operatorname{Ran}(P_L)
\]
is the exact family space.
A localized family basis \(\{\phi_a\}\) is only a \(U(3)\) choice inside this space.

For well-separated defects, the translated single-defect modes
\[
\psi_a(x)=\psi_\star(x-X_a)
\]
span an approximate low subspace whose projector converges exponentially to \(P_L\).

After this spectral splitting, one computes the perturbation blocks
\[
V_{LL}^{(I)},\qquad V_{LH}^{(I)},\qquad V_{HH}^{(I)},
\]
and obtains the reduced operator by Schur complement:
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal Y_{\rm eff}
=
L^{(0)}+V_{LL}-V_{LH}(H^{(0)}+V_{HH})^{-1}V_{HL}.
}
\]

To second order,
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal Y_{\rm eff}
=
L^{(0)}+V_{LL}-V_{LH}(H^{(0)})^{-1}V_{HL}+\mathcal O(\epsilon^3).
}
\]

If the heavy basis diagonalizes \(H^{(0)}\), then
\[
(\mathcal Y_{\rm eff}^{(2)})_{ab}
=
-
\sum_{I,J}\epsilon_I\epsilon_J
\sum_\alpha
\frac{K_{a\alpha}^{(I)}\overline{K_{b\alpha}^{(J)}}}{E_\alpha},
\]
so the CP-odd coefficients are
\[
\boxed{
a_{ab}^{(2)}
=
-
\sum_{I,J}\epsilon_I\epsilon_J
\sum_\alpha
\frac{
\Im\!\left(K_{a\alpha}^{(I)}\overline{K_{b\alpha}^{(J)}}\right)
}{E_\alpha}.
}
\]

Thus the family problem is now fully organized.
What remains is not conceptual but TECT-specific:
write the actual defect operator, solve the single-defect mode problem, build the three-defect projector, and evaluate the overlap matrix elements.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Minimal single-defect reference operator and the status of the one-mode assumption}

We now specify the minimal principled form of the single-defect reference operator
that should underlie the reduced TECT flavor construction.

\section{Why a first-order bundle operator is the natural reference choice}

At the reduced flavor stage, the reference operator should satisfy four requirements:

\begin{enumerate}
\item it must be self-adjoint, so that spectral projectors and Schur complements are well-defined;
\item it must admit localized defect-bound states;
\item it must naturally separate light and heavy sectors;
\item it must allow topological protection of low modes, if such protection is present.
\end{enumerate}

The mathematically natural minimal class satisfying these requirements is a first-order Dirac/Callias-type operator on a bundle over \(\mathbb R^3\):
\[
\boxed{
\mathbb O_{\rm 1def}^{(0)}
=
i\Gamma^i\bigl(\partial_i+\Omega_i^{\rm def}(x)\bigr)
+
\Phi_{\rm def}(x).
}
\]

Here:

\begin{itemize}
\item \(\Gamma^i\) are Hermitian Clifford generators acting on an internal fiber;
\item \(\Omega_i^{\rm def}(x)\) is a defect-induced connection-type field;
\item \(\Phi_{\rm def}(x)\) is a Hermitian endomorphism (mass/Higgs-type field) determined by the single-defect background.
\end{itemize}

This is the minimal reference form because:

\begin{enumerate}
\item it is compatible with self-adjoint elliptic theory;
\item it is the standard setting for localized zero modes bound to topological defects;
\item it fits naturally with the later light/heavy block elimination;
\item it isolates the topological input in the asymptotic structure of \(\Phi_{\rm def}\).
\end{enumerate}

\section{Abstract assumptions under which one localized light mode can be justified}

We now separate what can be established abstractly from what remains TECT-specific.

Assume the following.

\paragraph{Assumption A1 (self-adjoint Callias form).}
The operator
\[
\mathbb O_{\rm 1def}^{(0)}
=
i\Gamma^i(\partial_i+\Omega_i^{\rm def})+\Phi_{\rm def}
\]
is essentially self-adjoint on a natural core and defines a Fredholm operator after suitable domain completion.

\paragraph{Assumption A2 (asymptotic gap).}
There exists \(R_0>0\) and \(m_\infty>0\) such that for \(|x|>R_0\),
\[
\Phi_{\rm def}(x)^2 \ge m_\infty^2\,\mathbf 1.
\]
Equivalently, the defect background approaches a gapped bulk at infinity.

\paragraph{Assumption A3 (topological nontriviality).}
The asymptotic map
\[
\widehat{\Phi}_{\rm def}(x)
:=
\frac{\Phi_{\rm def}(x)}{|\Phi_{\rm def}(x)|}
\qquad (|x|\to\infty)
\]
defines a nontrivial topological class on the sphere at infinity.

\paragraph{Assumption A4 (index one).}
The associated Callias-type index is
\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{Ind}\bigl(\mathbb O_{\rm 1def}^{(0)}\bigr)=1.
}
\]

Under these assumptions, the single-defect problem has exactly one protected zero mode (counted with sign/chirality in the appropriate grading).

\section{Abstract theorem-level consequence}

Under A1--A4, one can state the following abstract theorem.

\paragraph{Theorem (abstract single-defect one-mode criterion).}
Let
\[
\mathbb O_{\rm 1def}^{(0)}
=
i\Gamma^i(\partial_i+\Omega_i^{\rm def})+\Phi_{\rm def}
\]
satisfy A1--A4.
Then:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the essential spectrum is gapped away from zero by the asymptotic gap;
\item zero is an isolated eigenvalue of finite multiplicity;
\item the defect supports exactly one normalizable protected light mode (in the index-counting sector);
\item this mode is exponentially localized.
\end{enumerate}

\paragraph{Reason.}
A2 implies that outside a compact region the operator is asymptotically invertible, so the essential spectrum is separated from zero.
A1 and A3 place the problem in the standard Callias/Fredholm framework.
A4 fixes the net number of protected zero modes to one.
Standard Agmon-type estimates then yield exponential localization of isolated defect-bound states.
\(\square\)

This theorem is abstract but rigorous once the assumptions are verified.

\section{What is genuinely proven and what is not}

It is crucial to distinguish the following two levels.

\subsection{What is abstractly closed}

If the TECT defect operator can be brought into the above Callias-type class and its index is one,
then the existence of one localized light mode per defect is mathematically justified.
Consequently, three well-separated defects naturally generate a three-dimensional light family sector.

So the following implication is rigorously valid:
\[
\boxed{
\text{Callias-type structure + asymptotic gap + index one}
\Longrightarrow
\text{one protected light mode per defect.}
}
\]

\subsection{What is still open and TECT-specific}

What has \emph{not} yet been established is that the actual TECT microscopic defect operator satisfies A1--A4.

More specifically, the following items remain open:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the exact microscopic linearized operator of TECT;
\item the precise identification of the connection term \(\Omega_i^{\rm def}\);
\item the precise identification of the endomorphism \(\Phi_{\rm def}\);
\item proof that the asymptotic bulk is invertible/gapped;
\item computation of the relevant topological class;
\item proof that the corresponding index is exactly \(1\).
\end{enumerate}

Therefore the statement
\[
\text{``one defect carries exactly one family mode''}
\]
is not yet a theorem of TECT itself.
At present it is an \emph{abstract theorem conditional on verifying A1--A4 for the TECT defect PDE.}

\section{Alternative second-order reference operator and why it is less fundamental}

One could also consider a second-order Schr\"odinger-type operator of the form
\[
\mathbb H_{\rm 1def}^{(0)}=-\Delta + U_{\rm def}(x).
\]
Such an operator can certainly support localized bound states and may be convenient for numerics.

However, it is less fundamental for the present structural program because:

\begin{enumerate}
\item it does not encode chirality/topological index as transparently;
\item zero-mode protection is less natural than in the first-order Callias framework;
\item the later CP-carrying overlap structure is more naturally expressed in a first-order bundle language.
\end{enumerate}

Thus a second-order effective operator may be useful as a derived auxiliary object,
but the correct minimal reference operator for the TECT flavor program should be first-order.

\section{Three-defect consequence if the single-defect criterion holds}

Suppose the abstract single-defect theorem applies and each isolated defect supports exactly one exponentially localized light mode
\[
\psi_\star(x-X_a),
\qquad a=1,2,3.
\]
Then for sufficiently well-separated defects, the three-defect reference operator
\[
\mathbb O_{\rm 3def}^{(0)}
\]
has an isolated low-energy three-state cluster,
and the exact light projector
\[
P_L
=
\frac{1}{2\pi i}\oint_\Gamma (z-\mathbb O_{\rm 3def}^{(0)})^{-1}dz
\]
has rank three.
Thus the family space
\[
\mathcal H_L=\operatorname{Ran}(P_L)
\]
is not assumed; it is derived.

This closes the abstract family-space construction, conditional on the single-defect theorem.

\section{Minimal TECT-specific target for the next derivation}

The real next task is therefore sharply defined:
derive the actual TECT linearized defect operator and show that it can be cast into the form
\[
\mathbb O_{\rm 1def}^{(0)}
=
i\Gamma^i(\partial_i+\Omega_i^{\rm def})+\Phi_{\rm def},
\]
then verify:

\begin{enumerate}
\item self-adjointness/Fredholmness;
\item asymptotic invertibility of \(\Phi_{\rm def}\);
\item the relevant topological charge;
\item the index value
\[
\operatorname{Ind}(\mathbb O_{\rm 1def}^{(0)})=1.
\]
\end{enumerate}

Only after this is done will the statement
\[
\text{``one defect } \leftrightarrow \text{ one family mode''}
\]
become a first-principles theorem inside TECT rather than a conditional structural hypothesis.

\section{Final summary}

The natural minimal reference operator for the single-defect TECT flavor problem is a first-order Dirac/Callias-type bundle operator
\[
\boxed{
\mathbb O_{\rm 1def}^{(0)}
=
i\Gamma^i(\partial_i+\Omega_i^{\rm def}(x))
+
\Phi_{\rm def}(x).
}
\]

This choice is structurally preferred because it is self-adjoint, supports spectral projectors, and is the standard framework for topologically protected localized defect modes.

At the abstract level, the following implication is rigorous:
\[
\boxed{
\text{self-adjoint Callias-type operator}
+
\text{asymptotic gap}
+
\text{nontrivial topological class}
+
\operatorname{Ind}=1
\Longrightarrow
\text{one exponentially localized protected light mode per defect.}
}
\]

Hence, if the TECT single-defect operator satisfies these conditions, then three well-separated defects naturally generate a rank-three light family projector.

What is not yet proved is that the actual TECT microscopic defect operator does satisfy these hypotheses.
Thus the statement
\[
\text{``one defect carries one family mode''}
\]
is currently a conditional theorem schema, not yet a derived theorem of TECT itself.

The correct next task is therefore:
\[
\boxed{
\text{derive the actual TECT linearized defect operator and verify the Callias-type hypotheses and index }1.
}
\]

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{From a microscopic TECT PDE to a Dirac/Callias-type reference operator}

We now formulate the precise theorem schema that connects a general microscopic TECT field equation to the single-defect reference operator used in the reduced flavor program.

\section{General first-order microscopic fluctuation equation}

Let
\[
\mathfrak X(t,x)
\]
denote the microscopic field multiplet of TECT, and let
\[
\mathfrak B_{\rm def}(x)
\]
be a static single-defect background solution of the full nonlinear field equations.

We consider small fluctuations
\[
\mathfrak X(t,x)=\mathfrak X_{\rm def}(x)+\psi(t,x),
\]
and assume that the linearized fluctuation equation takes the general first-order form
\[
\boxed{
i\,G^0_{\rm def}(x)\,\partial_t\psi
+
i\,G^i_{\rm def}(x)\,\partial_i\psi
+
M_{\rm def}(x)\,\psi
=0.
}
\]
Here:

\begin{itemize}
\item \(G^0_{\rm def}(x)\) is the time-kinetic Hermitian matrix;
\item \(G^i_{\rm def}(x)\) are the spatial principal-symbol matrices;
\item \(M_{\rm def}(x)\) is the defect-induced zeroth-order endomorphism.
\end{itemize}

This is the minimal general linearized form for a first-order microscopic PDE.

\section{Structural assumptions}

We now state the assumptions under which this linearized equation can be cast into the desired Dirac/Callias-type form.

\paragraph{Assumption B1 (positive kinetic metric).}
For every \(x\),
\[
G^0_{\rm def}(x)=G^0_{\rm def}(x)^\dagger,
\qquad
G^0_{\rm def}(x)>0.
\]

\paragraph{Assumption B2 (Hermitian symmetric-hyperbolic structure).}
The spatial coefficient matrices satisfy
\[
G^i_{\rm def}(x)=G^i_{\rm def}(x)^\dagger,
\]
and the linearized evolution is symmetric-hyperbolic with respect to the inner product induced by \(G^0_{\rm def}\).

\paragraph{Assumption B3 (regularity and asymptotic bulk limit).}
There exist bulk-limit matrices
\[
G^0_\infty,\qquad G^i_\infty,\qquad M_\infty
\]
such that
\[
G^0_{\rm def}(x)\to G^0_\infty,\qquad
G^i_{\rm def}(x)\to G^i_\infty,\qquad
M_{\rm def}(x)\to M_\infty
\qquad (|x|\to\infty),
\]
with sufficient decay of the defect perturbation.

\paragraph{Assumption B4 (bulk gap).}
The asymptotic bulk operator is gapped at zero.
Equivalently, after reduction to canonical form below, the asymptotic mass endomorphism is invertible.

These assumptions are abstract and do not yet use any special TECT details.

\section{Canonical Hermitian reduction}

Define the positive Hermitian square root
\[
S(x):=\bigl(G^0_{\rm def}(x)\bigr)^{1/2}.
\]
Introduce the canonically normalized fluctuation
\[
\chi(t,x):=S(x)\,\psi(t,x).
\]
Since
\[
\psi=S^{-1}\chi,
\]
the fluctuation equation becomes
\[
i\partial_t \chi
=
\mathbb H_{\rm def}\chi,
\]
with
\[
\boxed{
\mathbb H_{\rm def}
=
-i\,\Gamma^i_{\rm def}(x)\,\partial_i
+
W_{\rm def}(x),
}
\]
where
\[
\boxed{
\Gamma^i_{\rm def}(x)
=
S(x)^{-1}G^i_{\rm def}(x)S(x)^{-1},
}
\]
and
\[
\boxed{
W_{\rm def}(x)
=
-i\,S^{-1}G^i_{\rm def}\,(\partial_i S^{-1})
-
S^{-1}M_{\rm def}S^{-1}.
}
\]

This is an exact algebraic identity.

\paragraph{Proof.}
Substitute \(\psi=S^{-1}\chi\) into
\[
iG^0_{\rm def}\partial_t\psi+iG^i_{\rm def}\partial_i\psi+M_{\rm def}\psi=0.
\]
Using
\[
G^0_{\rm def}=S^2
\]
and
\[
\partial_i(S^{-1}\chi)=(\partial_iS^{-1})\chi+S^{-1}\partial_i\chi,
\]
one obtains
\[
iS\partial_t\chi
+
iG^i_{\rm def}(\partial_iS^{-1})\chi
+
iG^i_{\rm def}S^{-1}\partial_i\chi
+
M_{\rm def}S^{-1}\chi
=0.
\]
Multiplying by \(S^{-1}\) from the left yields
\[
i\partial_t\chi
=
-i\,S^{-1}G^i_{\rm def}S^{-1}\partial_i\chi
-
i\,S^{-1}G^i_{\rm def}(\partial_iS^{-1})\chi
-
S^{-1}M_{\rm def}S^{-1}\chi,
\]
which is exactly the announced formula.
\(\square\)

\section{Dirac/Callias-type decomposition}

The operator
\[
\mathbb H_{\rm def}
=
-i\,\Gamma^i_{\rm def}(x)\,\partial_i+W_{\rm def}(x)
\]
is already in first-order Hermitian form.
To cast it into the geometric form used previously, one writes
\[
\boxed{
\mathbb O_{\rm 1def}^{(0)}
=
i\,\widetilde\Gamma^i_{\rm def}(x)\bigl(\partial_i+\Omega_i^{\rm def}(x)\bigr)
+
\Phi_{\rm def}(x),
}
\]
where
\[
\widetilde\Gamma^i_{\rm def}:=-\Gamma^i_{\rm def},
\]
and the zeroth-order term is split as
\[
W_{\rm def}
=
i\,\widetilde\Gamma^i_{\rm def}\Omega_i^{\rm def}
+
\Phi_{\rm def}.
\]

This splitting is not unique until one chooses a bundle frame and a covariant derivative convention.
However, once such a convention is fixed, the decomposition has a natural interpretation:

\begin{itemize}
\item \(\widetilde\Gamma^i_{\rm def}\) is the defect-renormalized principal symbol;
\item \(\Omega_i^{\rm def}\) is the induced connection term;
\item \(\Phi_{\rm def}\) is the Hermitian endomorphism (mass/Higgs-type field).
\end{itemize}

Thus the Dirac/Callias-type reference form is not a guess:
it is the canonical first-order Hermitian form of the linearized microscopic PDE.

\section{Bulk limit and asymptotic mass gap}

Assume the defect background approaches a bulk vacuum at infinity.
Then
\[
\Gamma^i_{\rm def}(x)\to \Gamma^i_\infty,
\qquad
W_{\rm def}(x)\to W_\infty,
\qquad
\Phi_{\rm def}(x)\to \Phi_\infty
\qquad (|x|\to\infty).
\]

If
\[
\boxed{
\det \Phi_\infty \neq 0,
}
\]
then zero lies in a bulk gap.
This is the precise spectral meaning of the asymptotic gap assumption.

Under standard elliptic/Fredholm hypotheses, this implies that zero can only occur as an isolated defect-bound state of finite multiplicity.

\section{When a Callias-type index exists}

To define a protected index, one needs an additional grading structure.

Assume there exists a Hermitian involution
\[
\mathcal C=\mathcal C^\dagger,
\qquad
\mathcal C^2=1,
\]
such that
\[
\{\mathcal C,\widetilde\Gamma^i_{\rm def}\}=0,
\qquad
[\mathcal C,\Omega_i^{\rm def}]=0,
\qquad
\{\mathcal C,\Phi_{\rm def}\}=0.
\]

Then the operator is odd with respect to the grading \(\mathcal C\) and decomposes into chiral blocks
\[
\mathbb O_{\rm 1def}^{(0)}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & \mathbb O_-\\
\mathbb O_+ & 0
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

If, in addition, \(\Phi_\infty\) is invertible, the operator falls into the standard Callias-type Fredholm class.
Then one may define
\[
\operatorname{Ind}(\mathbb O_+)=\dim\ker \mathbb O_+ - \dim\ker \mathbb O_-.
\]

Abstractly, if
\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{Ind}(\mathbb O_+)=1,
}
\]
then there is exactly one protected light mode in the relevant chiral sector.

So the correct theorem schema is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{first-order Hermitian linearization}
+
\text{asymptotic bulk gap}
+
\text{grading}
+
\text{Callias index }1
\Longrightarrow
\text{one protected single-defect light mode.}
}
\]

\section{Exponential localization}

Suppose zero (or \(\lambda_\star\) near zero) is an isolated eigenvalue inside the bulk gap.
Then standard Agmon-type estimates imply exponential localization:
\[
\boxed{
\|\psi_\star(x)\|
\le
C\,e^{-\kappa |x|}
}
\]
for every
\[
0<\kappa < m_\infty-|\lambda_\star|,
\]
where \(m_\infty\) is the bulk spectral gap scale determined by \(\Phi_\infty\).

Thus the single-defect mode, if it exists, is not only normalizable but exponentially localized.
This is exactly what is required for the later three-defect family projector construction.

\section{What is abstractly closed}

Under B1--B4 and the grading/index hypotheses above, the following implications are rigorously valid:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the linearized microscopic PDE can be canonically reduced to the form
\[
i\partial_t\chi=\mathbb H_{\rm def}\chi,
\qquad
\mathbb H_{\rm def}
=
i\widetilde\Gamma^i_{\rm def}(\partial_i+\Omega_i^{\rm def})+\Phi_{\rm def};
\]

\item the asymptotic bulk gap is controlled by the invertibility of \(\Phi_\infty\);

\item isolated defect-bound light modes are exponentially localized;

\item if the corresponding Callias index is \(1\), then a single defect supports exactly one protected light mode in the relevant sector.
\end{enumerate}

So the reference operator program is mathematically coherent.

\section{What is still TECT-specific and open}

The following items are not closed abstractly and must be derived from the actual TECT microscopic PDE:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the precise microscopic field content \(\mathfrak X\);
\item the actual linearized coefficients \(G^0_{\rm def},G^i_{\rm def},M_{\rm def}\);
\item the concrete identification of \(\widetilde\Gamma^i_{\rm def},\Omega_i^{\rm def},\Phi_{\rm def}\);
\item proof that the asymptotic bulk is genuinely gapped;
\item proof that a grading \(\mathcal C\) exists;
\item computation of the actual index, and specifically verification that it equals \(1\).
\end{enumerate}

Therefore, at the present stage, the statement
\[
\text{``one TECT defect carries one family mode''}
\]
is still conditional.
It becomes a theorem of TECT only after these six items are checked.

\section{Minimal practical template for the actual TECT derivation}

Once the real TECT PDE is written, the correct derivation pipeline is:

\begin{enumerate}
\item find a static single-defect solution \(\mathfrak B_{\rm def}(x)\);
\item linearize the microscopic equations around it and read off
\[
G^0_{\rm def},\quad G^i_{\rm def},\quad M_{\rm def};
\]
\item perform the canonical reduction with
\[
S=(G^0_{\rm def})^{1/2};
\]
\item identify the induced
\[
\widetilde\Gamma^i_{\rm def},\quad \Omega_i^{\rm def},\quad \Phi_{\rm def};
\]
\item compute the asymptotic bulk limit and check
\[
\det \Phi_\infty\neq 0;
\]
\item identify the grading \(\mathcal C\), if present;
\item compute the corresponding index and test whether it equals \(1\).
\end{enumerate}

Only after that does the family-projector construction become a first-principles theorem of TECT itself.

\section{Final summary}

The correct minimal route from a microscopic TECT PDE to the single-defect reference operator is as follows.

Start from the linearized fluctuation equation around a static defect background:
\[
i\,G^0_{\rm def}(x)\,\partial_t\psi
+
i\,G^i_{\rm def}(x)\,\partial_i\psi
+
M_{\rm def}(x)\psi
=0,
\]
with \(G^0_{\rm def}>0\) Hermitian.

After the canonical field redefinition
\[
\chi=(G^0_{\rm def})^{1/2}\psi,
\]
this becomes
\[
i\partial_t\chi
=
-i\,\Gamma^i_{\rm def}(x)\partial_i\chi
+
W_{\rm def}(x)\chi,
\]
where
\[
\Gamma^i_{\rm def}
=
(G^0_{\rm def})^{-1/2}G^i_{\rm def}(G^0_{\rm def})^{-1/2},
\]
\[
W_{\rm def}
=
-i\,(G^0_{\rm def})^{-1/2}G^i_{\rm def}\,\partial_i(G^0_{\rm def})^{-1/2}
-
(G^0_{\rm def})^{-1/2}M_{\rm def}(G^0_{\rm def})^{-1/2}.
\]

Equivalently, after choosing a bundle frame and covariant derivative convention, one obtains the Dirac/Callias-type reference form
\[
\boxed{
\mathbb O_{\rm 1def}^{(0)}
=
i\,\widetilde\Gamma^i_{\rm def}(\partial_i+\Omega_i^{\rm def})
+
\Phi_{\rm def}.
}
\]

If the asymptotic bulk mass endomorphism is invertible,
\[
\det \Phi_\infty\neq 0,
\]
then zero lies in a bulk gap.
If, furthermore, a grading exists and the corresponding Callias index is
\[
\operatorname{Ind}(\mathbb O_+)=1,
\]
then a single defect supports exactly one protected exponentially localized light mode.

Therefore the statement
\[
\text{``one defect } \leftrightarrow \text{ one family mode''}
\]
is rigorously valid provided these operator-theoretic hypotheses hold.

What remains open is entirely TECT-specific:
derive the actual linearized coefficients from the microscopic TECT PDE,
identify \(\widetilde\Gamma^i_{\rm def},\Omega_i^{\rm def},\Phi_{\rm def}\),
check the bulk gap, and compute the relevant index.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Minimal microscopic action template for the TECT flavor program}

We now formulate the minimal microscopic action/PDE ansatz that is sufficient to generate the defect reference operator used in the reduced flavor program.

\section{Why a first-order Hermitian fluctuation system is the correct minimal template}

The reduced flavor program requires a microscopic fluctuation operator with the following properties:

\begin{enumerate}
\item a well-defined self-adjoint time evolution;
\item a natural light/heavy spectral decomposition;
\item compatibility with defect-localized bound states;
\item the possibility of a topological index for protected light modes.
\end{enumerate}

The minimal general structure that satisfies all four requirements is a first-order Hermitian quadratic action for a fluctuation multiplet \(\Psi\):
\[
\boxed{
S[\Psi;\mathfrak B]
=
\int dt\,d^3x\;
\left[
\frac{i}{2}\Psi^\dagger K^0(\mathfrak B)\!\stackrel{\leftrightarrow}{\partial_t}\!\Psi
+
\frac{i}{2}\Psi^\dagger K^i(\mathfrak B)\!\stackrel{\leftrightarrow}{\partial_i}\!\Psi
-
\Psi^\dagger \mathcal M(\mathfrak B)\Psi
\right].
}
\]

Here:

\begin{itemize}
\item \(\mathfrak B\) denotes the microscopic background fields of TECT;
\item \(\Psi\) is the fluctuation multiplet on the relevant internal bundle;
\item \(K^0(\mathfrak B)\) is the kinetic Hermitian matrix;
\item \(K^i(\mathfrak B)\) are the spatial principal-symbol matrices;
\item \(\mathcal M(\mathfrak B)\) is the Hermitian zeroth-order endomorphism.
\end{itemize}

This action is not yet claimed to be the unique TECT action.
It is the minimal operator-theoretic template that can support the later spectral construction.

\section{Euler--Lagrange equation of the quadratic first-order action}

Varying the action with respect to \(\Psi^\dagger\), one obtains
\[
\delta S
=
\int dt\,d^3x\;
\delta\Psi^\dagger
\left[
iK^0(\mathfrak B)\partial_t\Psi
+
iK^i(\mathfrak B)\partial_i\Psi
+
\mathcal N(\mathfrak B,\partial\mathfrak B)\Psi
\right],
\]
where \(\mathcal N\) includes the zeroth-order mass term and also the derivative contributions coming from integration by parts when the coefficients vary in space.

More explicitly, the Euler--Lagrange equation can always be written in the canonical first-order form
\[
\boxed{
i\,G^0(\mathfrak B,x)\,\partial_t\Psi
+
i\,G^i(\mathfrak B,x)\,\partial_i\Psi
+
M(\mathfrak B,\partial\mathfrak B;x)\,\Psi
=
0,
}
\]
with
\[
G^0=K^0,
\qquad
G^i=K^i,
\]
and where \(M\) contains both the original \(\mathcal M\) and the lower-order pieces induced by derivatives of \(K^i\).

Thus the desired linearized PDE is not an extra assumption:
it is the universal Euler--Lagrange form of the first-order Hermitian quadratic action.

\section{Expansion around a static defect background}

Let
\[
\mathfrak B_{\rm def}(x)
\]
be a static single-defect solution of the full microscopic TECT equations.

Write the full microscopic field as
\[
\mathfrak B(t,x)=\mathfrak B_{\rm def}(x)+\delta\mathfrak B(t,x),
\]
and expand the quadratic fluctuation action around \(\mathfrak B_{\rm def}\).
Then the fluctuation multiplet \(\psi\) satisfies
\[
\boxed{
i\,G^0_{\rm def}(x)\,\partial_t\psi
+
i\,G^i_{\rm def}(x)\,\partial_i\psi
+
M_{\rm def}(x)\psi
=
0,
}
\]
where
\[
G^0_{\rm def}(x):=G^0(\mathfrak B_{\rm def}(x),x),
\qquad
G^i_{\rm def}(x):=G^i(\mathfrak B_{\rm def}(x),x),
\]
\[
M_{\rm def}(x):=M(\mathfrak B_{\rm def}(x),\partial\mathfrak B_{\rm def}(x);x).
\]

This is exactly the reference fluctuation equation needed for the single-defect spectral problem.

\section{Precise formulas for } G^0_{\rm def},\,G^i_{\rm def},\,M_{\rm def}

From the action
\[
S=
\int dt\,d^3x\;
\left[
\frac{i}{2}\Psi^\dagger K^0\!\stackrel{\leftrightarrow}{\partial_t}\!\Psi
+
\frac{i}{2}\Psi^\dagger K^i\!\stackrel{\leftrightarrow}{\partial_i}\!\Psi
-
\Psi^\dagger \mathcal M\Psi
\right],
\]
the field equation is
\[
iK^0\partial_t\Psi+iK^i\partial_i\Psi
+
\left(
\mathcal M
+\frac{i}{2}\partial_iK^i
+\frac{i}{2}\partial_tK^0
\right)\Psi
=0.
\]

For a static background,
\[
\partial_tK^0(\mathfrak B_{\rm def})=0,
\]
so
\[
\boxed{
G^0_{\rm def}=K^0(\mathfrak B_{\rm def}),
\qquad
G^i_{\rm def}=K^i(\mathfrak B_{\rm def}),
}
\]
and
\[
\boxed{
M_{\rm def}
=
\mathcal M(\mathfrak B_{\rm def})
+\frac{i}{2}\partial_iK^i(\mathfrak B_{\rm def}).
}
\]

Thus the three objects that enter the defect spectral problem are read directly from the quadratic action.

\section{Canonical normalization and reference Hamiltonian}

Assume
\[
G^0_{\rm def}(x)=G^0_{\rm def}(x)^\dagger>0.
\]
Define
\[
S(x):=\bigl(G^0_{\rm def}(x)\bigr)^{1/2},
\qquad
\chi:=S\psi.
\]
Then the fluctuation equation becomes
\[
i\partial_t\chi=\mathbb H_{\rm def}\chi,
\]
with
\[
\boxed{
\mathbb H_{\rm def}
=
-i\,\Gamma^i_{\rm def}(x)\partial_i
+
W_{\rm def}(x),
}
\]
where
\[
\boxed{
\Gamma^i_{\rm def}
=
S^{-1}G^i_{\rm def}S^{-1},
}
\]
and
\[
\boxed{
W_{\rm def}
=
-i\,S^{-1}G^i_{\rm def}\,\partial_i(S^{-1})
-
S^{-1}M_{\rm def}S^{-1}.
}
\]

This is the exact canonical Hermitian Hamiltonian associated with the defect background.

\section{Dirac/Callias decomposition}

After fixing a bundle frame and covariant-derivative convention, the Hamiltonian may be written as
\[
\boxed{
\mathbb O_{\rm 1def}^{(0)}
=
i\widetilde\Gamma^i_{\rm def}(\partial_i+\Omega_i^{\rm def})
+
\Phi_{\rm def},
}
\]
with
\[
\widetilde\Gamma^i_{\rm def}:=-\Gamma^i_{\rm def},
\]
and
\[
W_{\rm def}=i\widetilde\Gamma^i_{\rm def}\Omega_i^{\rm def}+\Phi_{\rm def}.
\]

Thus the desired reference operator is not guessed:
it is the canonically normalized first-order fluctuation operator of the microscopic defect background.

\section{Minimal theorem schema for one protected defect mode}

Suppose the following hold:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \(G^0_{\rm def}>0\), so the canonical Hamiltonian is well-defined;
\item the asymptotic bulk limit exists:
\[
\widetilde\Gamma^i_{\rm def}(x)\to \widetilde\Gamma^i_\infty,
\qquad
\Phi_{\rm def}(x)\to \Phi_\infty
\qquad (|x|\to\infty);
\]
\item the asymptotic mass endomorphism is invertible:
\[
\det \Phi_\infty\neq 0;
\]
\item there exists a grading \(\mathcal C\) such that the operator is of Callias type;
\item the corresponding index is
\[
\operatorname{Ind}(\mathbb O_+)=1.
\]
\end{enumerate}

Then:

\begin{enumerate}
\item zero lies in a bulk gap;
\item the defect supports one protected normalizable light mode;
\item that mode is exponentially localized;
\item three well-separated defects generate a rank-three low spectral cluster.
\end{enumerate}

Therefore the family-space construction follows once these operator-theoretic hypotheses are verified.

\section{What is abstractly derived and what is still open}

The following are now abstractly derived:

\begin{enumerate}
\item a first-order Hermitian quadratic microscopic action yields the canonical fluctuation PDE
\[
iG^0_{\rm def}\partial_t\psi+iG^i_{\rm def}\partial_i\psi+M_{\rm def}\psi=0;
\]

\item the coefficients are read directly as
\[
G^0_{\rm def}=K^0(\mathfrak B_{\rm def}),
\qquad
G^i_{\rm def}=K^i(\mathfrak B_{\rm def}),
\qquad
M_{\rm def}=\mathcal M(\mathfrak B_{\rm def})+\frac{i}{2}\partial_iK^i(\mathfrak B_{\rm def});
\]

\item canonical normalization produces the reference operator
\[
\mathbb O_{\rm 1def}^{(0)}
=
i\widetilde\Gamma^i_{\rm def}(\partial_i+\Omega_i^{\rm def})+\Phi_{\rm def}.
\]
\end{enumerate}

The following remain TECT-specific and open:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the actual microscopic field multiplet \(\mathfrak B\);
\item the actual coefficient matrices \(K^0,K^i,\mathcal M\);
\item the explicit defect background \(\mathfrak B_{\rm def}\);
\item the asymptotic bulk gap;
\item the existence of a grading;
\item the actual index value, and in particular whether it equals \(1\).
\end{enumerate}

So the operator-theoretic skeleton is now fixed.
What remains is to insert the actual TECT microscopic model into that skeleton.

\section{Final summary}

A natural minimal microscopic ansatz for the TECT flavor program is a first-order Hermitian quadratic fluctuation action
\[
\boxed{
S[\Psi;\mathfrak B]
=
\int dt\,d^3x\;
\left[
\frac{i}{2}\Psi^\dagger K^0(\mathfrak B)\!\stackrel{\leftrightarrow}{\partial_t}\!\Psi
+
\frac{i}{2}\Psi^\dagger K^i(\mathfrak B)\!\stackrel{\leftrightarrow}{\partial_i}\!\Psi
-
\Psi^\dagger \mathcal M(\mathfrak B)\Psi
\right].
}
\]

Around a static defect background \(\mathfrak B_{\rm def}\), this yields the canonical linearized PDE
\[
\boxed{
i\,G^0_{\rm def}\partial_t\psi
+
i\,G^i_{\rm def}\partial_i\psi
+
M_{\rm def}\psi
=
0,
}
\]
with
\[
G^0_{\rm def}=K^0(\mathfrak B_{\rm def}),
\qquad
G^i_{\rm def}=K^i(\mathfrak B_{\rm def}),
\qquad
M_{\rm def}
=
\mathcal M(\mathfrak B_{\rm def})
+\frac{i}{2}\partial_iK^i(\mathfrak B_{\rm def}).
\]

After canonical normalization by
\[
\chi=(G^0_{\rm def})^{1/2}\psi,
\]
one obtains the Hermitian first-order defect operator
\[
\boxed{
\mathbb O_{\rm 1def}^{(0)}
=
i\widetilde\Gamma^i_{\rm def}(\partial_i+\Omega_i^{\rm def})
+
\Phi_{\rm def}.
}
\]

Therefore the desired reference operator is not guessed: it is the canonical defect fluctuation operator of the microscopic theory.

If, furthermore, the asymptotic bulk is gapped, a grading exists, and the corresponding Callias index equals \(1\), then a single defect carries exactly one protected exponentially localized light mode.

What remains open is entirely TECT-specific:
the actual microscopic field content, the actual coefficient matrices \(K^0,K^i,\mathcal M\), the explicit defect solution, the bulk gap, the grading, and the index computation.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Diagnostic review of the refined locked + Class II microscopic extraction branch}

The proposed branch is structurally correct and is presently the strongest explicit working microscopic route.
However, as written, it is not yet fully sufficient to justify the phrase
\[
\text{``actual exact microscopic coefficient extraction.''}
\]
It should instead be presented as the strongest currently available \emph{working extraction branch}, with several points made more precise.

\section{Step 1: microscopic branch --- what is correct and what must be clarified}

The expression
\[
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{mic}}
=
\mathcal L_{\Psi}^{\mathrm{ref}}[\Psi,P]
+\frac{M_X^2}{2}X_i^aX_i^a
+\alpha_X X_i^a J_i^a[\Psi,P]
+\beta_X X_i^a K_i^a[P,\Psi]
\]
is a microscopic Lagrangian density (or action density), not yet a PDE.
So the wording should be:
\[
\boxed{
\text{``We fix the strongest explicit microscopic action branch currently available.''}
}
\]

Moreover, the notation must be unified:
\[
\Psi \quad \text{vs.} \quad \psi
\]
should not be mixed.

Also, with no kinetic term for \(X_i^a\), the current branch should be interpreted as a heavy auxiliary mediator truncation.
Then integrating out \(X\) is indeed exact, but only at the quadratic fluctuation level:
\[
\boxed{
\text{``integrated out exactly at the quadratic fluctuation level by Schur complement.''}
}
\]

Finally, the currents \(J_i^a\) and \(K_i^a\) must be specified precisely enough that their index structure, Hermiticity, and lattice-locking transformation properties are unambiguous.

\section{Step 2: Bloch expansion and Schur complement}

The Bloch reduction is the right move, but the operator should be defined as the quadratic fluctuation operator around the locked BCC branch, not just informally as a Hessian.

Let
\[
\mathfrak B_{\rm lock}
\]
denote the locked 12-mode BCC background, and let
\[
\delta\Phi=(\delta\Psi,\delta X)
\]
be the fluctuation multiplet.
Then the quadratic Bloch fiber operator is
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{Bloch}}(k)
=
\begin{pmatrix}
H_{\Psi\Psi}(k) & H_{\Psi X}(k)\\
H_{X\Psi}(k) & H_{XX}(k)
\end{pmatrix}.
}
\]

The exact quadratic effective operator after eliminating the heavy mediator is
\[
\boxed{
H_{\mathrm{eff}}(k)
=
H_{\Psi\Psi}(k)-H_{\Psi X}(k)\,H_{XX}(k)^{-1}\,H_{X\Psi}(k),
}
\]
provided \(H_{XX}(k)\) is invertible in the momentum region of interest.
Since the branch assumes
\[
M_X^2\gg 1,
\]
this invertibility is plausible, but should be stated explicitly.

The shell-projected operator is then
\[
\boxed{
H_{\mathrm{shell}}(p)
=
\Pi_{S_*}\,H_{\mathrm{eff}}(k_*+p)\,\Pi_{S_*},
}
\]
where \(k_*=G_*/2\) is the folded Bragg point and \(\Pi_{S_*}\) is the shell projector associated with the near-degenerate shell.

\section{Step 3: first-order transport matrix}

The first-order transport matrix should be written with the correct evaluation point:
\[
\boxed{
K_i
=
\left.\partial_{k_i}H_{\mathrm{eff}}(k)\right|_{k=k_*}.
}
\]

However, to make this mathematically clean, the low valley doublet must be spectrally isolated at \(p=0\).
Then the valley projector should be defined as the Riesz projector
\[
\boxed{
P_*
=
\frac{1}{2\pi i}\oint_\Gamma (z-H_{\mathrm{shell}}(0))^{-1}\,dz,
}
\]
where \(\Gamma\) encloses only the two low valley eigenvalues.

The linearized valley operator is then
\[
H_{\mathrm{val}}(p)
=
E_* P_*+\sum_i p_i M_i+\mathcal O(|p|^2),
\qquad
M_i:=P_*K_iP_*.
\]

This is the correct first-order Dirac block extraction.

\section{Step 4: defect-frame decomposition}

Given an adapted defect frame
\[
(\hat n,e_1,e_2),
\]
define
\[
M_\parallel:=\hat n_i M_i,
\qquad
M_1:=e_{1i}M_i,
\qquad
M_2:=e_{2i}M_i.
\]

At this stage, every \(M_\mu\) is an arbitrary Hermitian \(2\times2\) matrix, so one should first write the full Pauli expansion
\[
M_\mu=t_\mu \mathbf 1+\sum_{a=1}^3 v_\mu^a \sigma_a,
\qquad
v_\mu^a=\frac12\Tr(\sigma_a M_\mu).
\]

Only after imposing the locked branch symmetry may one reduce this to the special form
\[
\boxed{
M_\parallel=\lambda_\parallel \sigma_3,
\qquad
M_1=\alpha \sigma_1+\beta \sigma_2,
\qquad
M_2=\alpha \sigma_2-\beta \sigma_1.
}
\]

This is the exact place where the symmetry theorem must be cited or proved.

\section{Step 5: trace extraction of the reduced coefficients}

Once the special locked form above is established, the correct coefficient extraction is
\[
\boxed{
\lambda_\parallel=\frac12\Tr(\sigma_3 M_\parallel),
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\alpha=\frac14\Tr(\sigma_1 M_1+\sigma_2 M_2),
\qquad
\beta=\frac14\Tr(\sigma_2 M_1-\sigma_1 M_2).
}
\]

These formulas are always correct under the locked branch selection rule.
By contrast, the simpler equalities
\[
\alpha=\frac12\Tr(\sigma_1 M_1),
\qquad
\beta=\frac12\Tr(\sigma_2 M_1)
\]
are not automatic unless the additional locked relations have already been proved:
\[
\Tr(\sigma_2 M_2)=\Tr(\sigma_1 M_1),
\qquad
\Tr(\sigma_1 M_2)=-\Tr(\sigma_2 M_1),
\]
and all forbidden Pauli components vanish.

So the symmetric \(1/4\)-trace formulas are the safer exact expressions.

\section{Step 6: dictionary to }(g_c,g_E,g_O)

The proposed dictionary
\[
\lambda_\parallel=\frac{g_c}{2}I_{\theta'^3},
\qquad
\alpha=g_E I_\theta,
\qquad
\beta=g_O I_\theta
\]
is plausible as a working Class II specialization, but it should be written more cautiously.

The exact statement should first be
\[
\boxed{
\lambda_\parallel=C_c\,g_c,
\qquad
\alpha=C_E\,g_E,
\qquad
\beta=C_O\,g_O,
}
\]
where the coefficients \(C_c,C_E,C_O\) are explicit microscopic overlap integrals computed from the locked branch wavefunctions.

Only after an actual overlap evaluation may one conclude, if true, that
\[
C_c=\frac12 I_{\theta'^3},
\qquad
C_E=I_\theta^{(E)},
\qquad
C_O=I_\theta^{(O)},
\]
and only under additional symmetry can one set
\[
I_\theta^{(E)}=I_\theta^{(O)}=:I_\theta.
\]

So the current text is slightly too compressed at this point.

\section{Correct honest status}

The correct current status is therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the refined locked + Class II branch provides the strongest explicit microscopic working route to coefficient extraction,}
}
\]
but
\[
\boxed{
\text{it is not yet a fully closed exact extraction theorem until the projector, symmetry selection rules, and overlap dictionary are written completely explicitly.}
}
\]

In particular, the following items still need to be made explicit:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the exact definitions of \(J_i^a\) and \(K_i^a\);
\item the precise quadratic fluctuation operator about the locked background;
\item the spectral definition of \(P_*\);
\item the locked symmetry theorem enforcing the special Pauli structure of \(M_\parallel,M_1,M_2\);
\item the explicit overlap-integral derivation of \(C_c,C_E,C_O\).
\end{enumerate}

Once these are inserted, the branch becomes a clean and nearly submission-ready extraction framework.

\section{Final summary}

The proposed refined locked + Class II branch is structurally correct and is presently the strongest explicit microscopic working branch for coefficient extraction.

However, in its current form it should not yet be advertised as a fully exact extraction theorem.

The main points that still need to be made explicit are:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the branch is an action/Lagrangian branch, not yet a PDE;
\item the heavy mediator is integrated out exactly only at the quadratic fluctuation level;
\item the valley projector \(P_*\) should be defined spectrally as a Riesz projector;
\item the special Pauli structure of the projected matrices \(M_\parallel,M_1,M_2\) must be justified by the locked symmetry;
\item the coefficient dictionary to \((g_c,g_E,g_O)\) should first be written with general overlap constants \((C_c,C_E,C_O)\), and only later reduced to specific integrals once explicitly computed.
\end{enumerate}

Thus the correct honest statement is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{this branch already provides a very strong explicit working microscopic extraction framework,}
}
\]
but
\[
\boxed{
\text{it still requires a few precise definitions and symmetry statements before it becomes a fully closed exact extraction theorem.}
}
\]

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%


\section{Specialization of the abstract Schur-complement formalism to the refined locked + Class II working branch}

We now specialize the general light/heavy block formalism to the explicit microscopic working branch
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{mic}}
=
\mathcal L_{\Psi}^{\mathrm{ref}}[\Psi,P]
+\frac{M_X^2}{2}X_i^aX_i^a
+\alpha_X X_i^a J_i^a[\Psi,P]
+\beta_X X_i^a K_i^a[P,\Psi].
}
\]
This is not yet the full microscopic TECT action, but it is the strongest currently available explicit working branch for actual coefficient extraction.

\section{Stationary locked background and fluctuation variables}

Let
\[
(\bar\Psi,\bar P,\bar X)
\]
be a static locked BCC branch solving the stationarity conditions of the above working action.
Because \(X\) appears as a heavy mediator in the present truncation, its background value is determined algebraically by
\[
M_X^2 \bar X_i^a + \alpha_X J_i^a[\bar\Psi,\bar P]+\beta_X K_i^a[\bar P,\bar\Psi]=0.
\]
Thus
\[
\boxed{
\bar X_i^a
=
-\frac{\alpha_X J_i^a[\bar\Psi,\bar P]+\beta_X K_i^a[\bar P,\bar\Psi]}{M_X^2}.
}
\]

Now write the fluctuations
\[
\Psi=\bar\Psi+\delta\Psi,
\qquad
X=\bar X+\delta X.
\]
The quadratic fluctuation operator around the locked branch is the object that must be Bloch decomposed and shell projected.

\section{Quadratic Bloch fiber operator}

Pass to the reciprocal-harmonic Bloch basis near the folded Bragg point
\[
k_*=G_*/2,
\qquad
k=k_*+p.
\]
Let \(\delta\Phi=(\delta\Psi,\delta X)\).
Then the quadratic Bloch fiber operator takes the block form
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{Bloch}}(k)
=
\begin{pmatrix}
H_{\Psi\Psi}(k) & H_{\Psi X}(k)\\
H_{X\Psi}(k) & H_{XX}(k)
\end{pmatrix}.
}
\]

The blocks are defined as the second variations of the microscopic working branch at the locked background:
\[
H_{\Psi\Psi}(k)
=
\left.\frac{\delta^2 \mathcal L_{\rm mic}}{\delta\Psi^\dagger\,\delta\Psi}\right|_{\bar\Psi,\bar P,\bar X}^{\rm Bloch}(k),
\]
\[
H_{\Psi X}(k)
=
\left.\frac{\delta^2 \mathcal L_{\rm mic}}{\delta\Psi^\dagger\,\delta X}\right|_{\bar\Psi,\bar P,\bar X}^{\rm Bloch}(k),
\qquad
H_{X\Psi}(k)=H_{\Psi X}(k)^\dagger,
\]
\[
H_{XX}(k)
=
\left.\frac{\delta^2 \mathcal L_{\rm mic}}{\delta X\,\delta X}\right|_{\bar\Psi,\bar P,\bar X}^{\rm Bloch}(k).
\]

In the present Class II truncation, where \(X\) has no independent kinetic term at this stage,
\[
\boxed{
H_{XX}(k)=M_X^2\,\mathbf 1_X
}
\]
up to possible subleading background-induced algebraic corrections.
So at the working level \(H_{XX}\) is heavy and invertible.

\section{Explicit current vertices}

Define the linearized current vertices
\[
\mathcal J_{i}^{a}(k)
:=
\left.\frac{\delta J_i^a[\Psi,P]}{\delta\Psi}\right|_{\bar\Psi,\bar P}^{\rm Bloch}(k),
\qquad
\mathcal K_{i}^{a}(k)
:=
\left.\frac{\delta K_i^a[P,\Psi]}{\delta\Psi}\right|_{\bar P,\bar\Psi}^{\rm Bloch}(k).
\]

Then the mixed block is
\[
\boxed{
H_{\Psi X}(k)
=
\alpha_X\,\mathcal J(k)+\beta_X\,\mathcal K(k),
}
\]
where \(\mathcal J(k)\) and \(\mathcal K(k)\) denote the appropriately bundled collections of \(\mathcal J_i^a(k)\) and \(\mathcal K_i^a(k)\) over the mediator indices \((i,a)\).

Therefore the heavy-mediated correction is
\[
\boxed{
\Delta H_{\rm eff}(k)
=
-
\bigl(\alpha_X\mathcal J(k)+\beta_X\mathcal K(k)\bigr)\,
H_{XX}(k)^{-1}\,
\bigl(\alpha_X\mathcal J(k)+\beta_X\mathcal K(k)\bigr)^\dagger.
}
\]

If \(H_{XX}(k)=M_X^2\mathbf 1_X\), this simplifies to
\[
\boxed{
\Delta H_{\rm eff}(k)
=
-\frac{1}{M_X^2}
\bigl(\alpha_X\mathcal J(k)+\beta_X\mathcal K(k)\bigr)
\bigl(\alpha_X\mathcal J(k)+\beta_X\mathcal K(k)\bigr)^\dagger.
}
\]

Hence the full effective shell operator is
\[
\boxed{
H_{\rm eff}(k)
=
H_{\Psi\Psi}(k)+\Delta H_{\rm eff}(k).
}
\]

\section{Shell projection and exact valley projector}

Let \(\Pi_{S_*}\) denote the shell projector around the resonant folded shell \(S_*\).
Then
\[
\boxed{
H_{\rm shell}(p)
=
\Pi_{S_*}\,H_{\rm eff}(k_*+p)\,\Pi_{S_*}.
}
\]

Let \(\Gamma\) be a contour surrounding only the two low valley eigenvalues of \(H_{\rm shell}(0)\).
Then the exact valley projector is the Riesz projector
\[
\boxed{
P_*
=
\frac{1}{2\pi i}\oint_\Gamma (z-H_{\rm shell}(0))^{-1}dz.
}
\]

This is the correct basis-independent definition of the valley doublet.
Only after this spectral projection may one choose a Pauli basis \(\{\sigma_1,\sigma_2,\sigma_3\}\) on \(\operatorname{Ran}(P_*)\).

\section{First-order transport operator}

Define the first momentum derivative at the Bragg point
\[
\boxed{
K_i
=
\left.\partial_{k_i}H_{\rm eff}(k)\right|_{k=k_*}.
}
\]

Since
\[
H_{\rm eff}(k)=H_{\Psi\Psi}(k)-H_{\Psi X}(k)H_{XX}(k)^{-1}H_{X\Psi}(k),
\]
one obtains the exact derivative formula
\[
\boxed{
\partial_{k_i}H_{\rm eff}
=
\partial_{k_i}H_{\Psi\Psi}
-
(\partial_{k_i}H_{\Psi X})H_{XX}^{-1}H_{X\Psi}
-
H_{\Psi X}H_{XX}^{-1}(\partial_{k_i}H_{X\Psi})
+
H_{\Psi X}H_{XX}^{-1}(\partial_{k_i}H_{XX})H_{XX}^{-1}H_{X\Psi}.
}
\]

In the pure auxiliary Class II truncation,
\[
\partial_{k_i}H_{XX}=0,
\]
so this simplifies to
\[
\boxed{
\partial_{k_i}H_{\rm eff}
=
\partial_{k_i}H_{\Psi\Psi}
-
(\partial_{k_i}H_{\Psi X})H_{XX}^{-1}H_{X\Psi}
-
H_{\Psi X}H_{XX}^{-1}(\partial_{k_i}H_{X\Psi}).
}
\]

Projecting onto the valley subspace,
\[
\boxed{
M_i=P_*K_iP_*.
}
\]

This is the exact microscopic transport matrix entering the reduced Dirac block.

\section{Locked branch Pauli selection rule}

At this stage \(M_i\) is an arbitrary Hermitian \(2\times2\) matrix.
In an adapted defect frame
\[
(\hat n,e_1,e_2),
\]
define
\[
M_\parallel=\hat n_i M_i,
\qquad
M_1=e_{1i}M_i,
\qquad
M_2=e_{2i}M_i.
\]

The locked branch symmetry is precisely the statement that, after an appropriate Pauli basis choice on the valley doublet,
\[
\boxed{
M_\parallel=\lambda_\parallel \sigma_3,
\qquad
M_1=\alpha \sigma_1+\beta \sigma_2,
\qquad
M_2=\alpha \sigma_2-\beta \sigma_1.
}
\]

This is the exact reduced transport form that isolates the Dirac block.

\section{Trace extraction of }(\lambda_\parallel,\alpha,\beta)

Once the locked Pauli form is established, the reduced coefficients are extracted exactly by
\[
\boxed{
\lambda_\parallel=\frac12\Tr(\sigma_3 M_\parallel),
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\alpha
=
\frac14\Tr(\sigma_1 M_1+\sigma_2 M_2),
\qquad
\beta
=
\frac14\Tr(\sigma_2 M_1-\sigma_1 M_2).
}
\]

These formulas are exact under the locked symmetry selection rule.
